Many centrist Democrats and members of the party's leadership supported the use of torture while the liberal wings continued to be steadfastly opposed to it.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="USA_PATRIOT_Act">USA PATRIOT Act</span></h4>
<p>Many Democrats are opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Patriot_Act" title="Patriot Act">Patriot Act</a>, but when the law was passed most Democrats were supportive of it and all but two Democrats in the Senate voted for the original Patriot Act legislation in 2001. The lone nay vote was from <a href="/wiki/Russ_Feingold" title="Russ Feingold">Russ Feingold</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a> as <a href="/wiki/Mary_Landrieu" title="Mary Landrieu">Mary Landrieu</a> of <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> did not vote.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> In the House, the Democrats voted for the Act by 145 yea and 62 nay. Democrats split on the renewal in 2006. In the Senate, Democrats voted 34 for the 2006 renewal and 9 against. In the House, Democrats voted 66 voted for the renewal, and 124 against.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Right_to_privacy">Right to privacy</span></h4>
<p>The Democratic Party believes that individuals should have a <a href="/wiki/Privacy_law" title="Privacy law">right to privacy</a>. For example, many Democrats have opposed the <a href="/wiki/NSA_warrantless_surveillance_(2001%E2%80%9307)" class="mw-redirect" title="NSA warrantless surveillance (2001–07)">NSA warrantless surveillance of American citizens</a>.
</p><p>Some Democratic officeholders have championed <a href="/wiki/Consumer_protection" title="Consumer protection">consumer protection</a> laws that limit the sharing of consumer data between corporations. Most Democrats oppose <a href="/wiki/Sodomy_laws_in_the_United_States" title="Sodomy laws in the United States">sodomy laws</a> and believe that government should not regulate consensual noncommercial sexual conduct among adults as a matter of personal privacy.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy_issues">Foreign policy issues</span></h3>
<p>The foreign policy of the voters of the two major parties has largely overlapped since the 1990s. A Gallup poll in early 2013 showed broad agreement on the top issues, albeit with some divergence regarding human rights and international cooperation through agencies such as the United Nations.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2014, the Quinnipiac Poll asked Americans which foreign policy they preferred:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>A) The United States is doing too much in other countries around the world, and it is time to do less around the world and focus more on our own problems here at home.
</p><p>B) The United States must continue to push forward to promote democracy and freedom in other countries around the world because these efforts make our own country more secure.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Democrats chose A over B by 65% to 32%; Republicans chose A over B by 56% to 39%; and independents chose A over B by 67% to 29%.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iraq_War">Iraq War</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Barack_Obama_2008_Iraq_19.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Barack_Obama_2008_Iraq_19.jpg/160px-Barack_Obama_2008_Iraq_19.jpg" decoding="async" width="160" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Barack_Obama_2008_Iraq_19.jpg/240px-Barack_Obama_2008_Iraq_19.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Barack_Obama_2008_Iraq_19.jpg/320px-Barack_Obama_2008_Iraq_19.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1625" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Barack_Obama_2008_Iraq_19.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> shaking hands with an American soldier in <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> in 2008.</div></div></div>
<p>In 2002, Congressional Democrats were divided on the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_Resolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraq Resolution">Authorization for Use of Military Force Against Iraq</a>: 147 voted against it (21 in the Senate and 126 in the House) and 110 voted for it (29 in the Senate and 81 in the House). Since then, many prominent Democrats, such as former senator <a href="/wiki/John_Edwards" title="John Edwards">John Edwards</a>, have expressed regret about this decision and have called it a mistake while others, such as Senator <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a>, have criticized the conduct of the war yet not repudiated their initial vote for it (though Clinton later went on to repudiate her stance during the 2008 primaries). Referring to Iraq, <a href="/wiki/Party_leaders_of_the_United_States_Senate" title="Party leaders of the United States Senate">Senate Majority Leader</a> <a href="/wiki/Harry_Reid" title="Harry Reid">Harry Reid</a> declared in April 2007 the war to be "lost" while other Democrats (especially during the 2004 presidential election cycle) accused the President of lying to the public about weapons of mass destruction in Iraq. Among lawmakers, Democrats are the most vocal opponents of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Iraqi_Freedom_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Iraqi Freedom VI">Operation Iraqi Freedom</a> and campaigned on a platform of withdrawal ahead of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_elections,_2006" class="mw-redirect" title="United States elections, 2006">2006 midterm elections</a>.
</p><p>A March 2003 <a href="/wiki/CBS_News" title="CBS News">CBS News</a> poll taken a few days before the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">invasion of Iraq</a> found that 34% of Democrats nationwide would support it without <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> backing, 51% would support it only with its backing and 14% would not support it at all.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i> stated in early April 2003 that 70% of Democrats supported the decision to invade while 27% opposed it.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> stated in August 2007 that opposition increased from 37% during the initial invasion to 74%.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> In April 2008, a <a href="/wiki/CBS_News" title="CBS News">CBS News</a> poll found that about 90% of Democrats disapprove of the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_George_W._Bush" title="Presidency of George W. Bush">Bush administration</a>'s conduct and want to end the war within the next year.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Democrats in the House of Representatives near-unanimously supported a <a href="/wiki/Non-binding_resolution" title="Non-binding resolution">non-binding resolution</a> disapproving of President Bush's decision to send <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War_troop_surge_of_2007" title="Iraq War troop surge of 2007">additional troops into Iraq in 2007</a>. Congressional Democrats overwhelmingly supported military funding legislation that included a provision that set "a timeline for the withdrawal of all US combat troops from Iraq" by March 31, 2008, but also would leave combat forces in Iraq for purposes such as targeted counter-terrorism operations.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> After a veto from the President and a failed attempt in Congress to override the veto,<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Troop_Readiness,_Veterans%27_Care,_Katrina_Recovery,_and_Iraq_Accountability_Appropriations_Act,_2007" title="U.S. Troop Readiness, Veterans&#39; Care, Katrina Recovery, and Iraq Accountability Appropriations Act, 2007">U.S. Troop Readiness, Veterans' Care, Katrina Recovery, and Iraq Accountability Appropriations Act, 2007</a> was passed by Congress and signed by the President after the timetable was dropped. <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_the_Iraq_War" title="Criticism of the Iraq War">Criticism of the Iraq War</a> subsided after the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War_troop_surge_of_2007" title="Iraq War troop surge of 2007">Iraq War troop surge of 2007</a> led to a dramatic decrease in Iraqi violence. The Democratic-controlled 110th Congress continued to fund efforts in both Iraq and Afghanistan. Presidential candidate <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> advocated a withdrawal of combat troops within Iraq by late 2010 with a residual force of peacekeeping troops left in place.<sup id="cite_ref-force_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-force-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> He stated that both the speed of withdrawal and the number of troops left over would be "entirely conditions-based".<sup id="cite_ref-force_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-force-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On February 27, 2009, President Obama announced: "As a candidate for president, I made clear my support for a timeline of 16 months to carry out this drawdown, while pledging to consult closely with our military commanders upon taking office to ensure that we preserve the gains we've made and protect our troops&#160;[...] Those consultations are now complete, and I have chosen a timeline that will remove our combat brigades over the next 18 months".<sup id="cite_ref-top_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-top-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Around 50,000 non-combat-related forces would remain.<sup id="cite_ref-top_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-top-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Obama's plan drew wide bipartisan support, including that of defeated Republican presidential candidate Senator <a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-top_203-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-top-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (April 2017)">needs update</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Iran_sanctions">Iran sanctions</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/United_States_sanctions_against_Iran" title="United States sanctions against Iran">United States sanctions against Iran</a></div>
<p>The Democratic Party has been critical of the Iran's nuclear weapon program and supported economic sanctions against the Iranian government. In 2013, the Democratic-led administration worked to reach a diplomatic agreement with the government of Iran to halt the Iranian nuclear weapon program in exchange for <a href="/wiki/Sanctions_against_Iran" title="Sanctions against Iran">international economic sanction</a> relief.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Democratic_Party_(United_States)&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, negotiations had been successful and the party called for more cooperation with Iran in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, the Obama administration agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Comprehensive_Plan_of_Action" title="Joint Comprehensive Plan of Action">Joint Comprehensive Plan of Action</a>, which provides sanction relief in exchange for international oversight of the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_nuclear_program" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranian nuclear program">Iranian nuclear program</a>. In February 2019, the Democratic National Committee passed a resolution calling on the United States to re-enter the JCPOA, which President Trump withdrew from in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Invasion_of_Afghanistan">Invasion of Afghanistan</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan_%E2%80%93_United_States_relations" class="mw-redirect" title="Afghanistan – United States relations">Afghanistan – United States relations</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_public_opinion_on_the_war_in_Afghanistan" title="International public opinion on the war in Afghanistan">International public opinion on the war in Afghanistan</a></div>
<p>Democrats in the House of Representatives and in the Senate near-unanimously voted for the <a href="/wiki/Authorization_for_Use_of_Military_Force_Against_Terrorists" title="Authorization for Use of Military Force Against Terrorists">Authorization for Use of Military Force Against Terrorists</a> against "those responsible for the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">recent attacks launched against the United States</a>" in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> in 2001, supporting the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> coalition <a href="/wiki/Operation_Enduring_Freedom" title="Operation Enduring Freedom">invasion of the nation</a>. Most elected Democrats continue to support the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">Afghanistan conflict</a> and some, such as a <a href="/wiki/Democratic_National_Committee" title="Democratic National Committee">Democratic National Committee</a> spokesperson, have voiced concerns that the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of Iraq">Iraq War</a> shifted too many resources away from the presence in Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-CNS_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNS-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-daily_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daily-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Since 2006, Democratic candidate <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> has called for a "surge" of troops into Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-daily_209-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daily-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> As president, Obama sent a "surge" force of additional troops to Afghanistan. Troop levels were 94,000 in December 2011 and kept falling, with a target of 68,000 by fall 2012. Obama planned to bring all the troops home by 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Support for the war among the American people has diminished over time and many Democrats have changed their opinion and now oppose a continuation of the conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-holland_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-holland-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-edge_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edge-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> In July 2008, <a href="/wiki/Gallup_poll" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallup poll">Gallup</a> found that 41% of Democrats called the invasion a "mistake" while a 55% majority disagreed. In contrast, Republicans were more supportive of the war. The survey described Democrats as evenly divided about whether or not more troops should be sent—56% support it if it would mean removing troops from Iraq and only 47% support it otherwise.<sup id="cite_ref-edge_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edge-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">CNN</a> survey in August 2009 stated that a majority of Democrats now oppose the war. CNN polling director Keating Holland said: "Nearly two thirds of Republicans support the war in Afghanistan. Three quarters of Democrats oppose the war".<sup id="cite_ref-holland_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-holland-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> An August 2009 <i>Washington Post</i> poll found similar results and the paper stated that Obama's policies would anger his closest supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Israel">Israel</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Israel%E2%80%93United_States_relations" title="Israel–United States relations">Israel–United States relations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jimmy_Carter_and_Menahem_Begin_-_NARA_-_181332.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Jimmy_Carter_and_Menahem_Begin_-_NARA_-_181332.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Jimmy_Carter_and_Menahem_Begin_-_NARA_-_181332.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Jimmy_Carter_and_Menahem_Begin_-_NARA_-_181332.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Jimmy_Carter_and_Menahem_Begin_-_NARA_-_181332.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Jimmy_Carter_and_Menahem_Begin_-_NARA_-_181332.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Jimmy_Carter_and_Menahem_Begin_-_NARA_-_181332.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2047" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jimmy_Carter_and_Menahem_Begin_-_NARA_-_181332.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israeli</a> Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Menachem_Begin" title="Menachem Begin">Menachem Begin</a> in 1978.</div></div></div>
<p>The Democratic Party has both recently and historically supported <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Israel_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Israel-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-leftpush_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leftpush-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Former <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">House Speaker</a> <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Pelosi" title="Nancy Pelosi">Nancy Pelosi</a> said in 2009: "When it comes to Israel, Republicans and Democrats speak with one voice".<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> A 2008 <a href="/wiki/Gallup_poll" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallup poll">Gallup poll</a> found that 64% say that they have a favorable image of Israel while only 16% say that they have a favorable image of the <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_Authority" class="mw-redirect" title="Palestinian Authority">Palestinian Authority</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Israel_214-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Israel-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> Within the party, the majority view is held by the Democratic leadership although some members such as <a href="/wiki/John_Conyers_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="John Conyers Jr.">John Conyers Jr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Miller_(California_politician)" title="George Miller (California politician)">George Miller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nick_Rahall" title="Nick Rahall">Nick Rahall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dave_Obey" title="Dave Obey">Dave Obey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pete_Stark" title="Pete Stark">Pete Stark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Kucinich" title="Dennis Kucinich">Dennis Kucinich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jim_McDermott" title="Jim McDermott">Jim McDermott</a> as well as former <a href="/wiki/POTUS" class="mw-redirect" title="POTUS">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> are less or not supportive of Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-leftpush_215-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leftpush-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> The party leadership refers to the few Democrats unsympathetic to Israel as a "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fringe" class="extiw" title="wikt:fringe">fringe</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-leftpush_215-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leftpush-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2008 Democratic Party platform acknowledges a "<a href="/wiki/Special_relationship" class="mw-redirect" title="Special relationship">special relationship</a> with Israel, grounded in shared interests and shared values, and a clear, strong, fundamental commitment to the security of Israel, our strongest ally in the region and its only established democracy". It also included:
</p>
<blockquote><p>It is in the best interests of all parties, including the United States, that we take an active role to help secure a lasting settlement of the Israeli-Palestinian conflict with a democratic, viable Palestinian state dedicated to living in peace and security side by side with the Jewish State of Israel. To do so, we must help Israel identify and strengthen those partners who are truly committed to peace while isolating those who seek conflict and instability, and stand with Israel against those who seek its destruction. The United States and its Quartet partners should continue to isolate Hamas until it renounces terrorism, recognizes Israel's right to exist, and abides by past agreements. Sustained American leadership for peace and security will require patient efforts and the personal commitment of the President of the United States. The creation of a Palestinian state through final status negotiations, together with an international compensation mechanism, should resolve the issue of Palestinian refugees by allowing them to settle there, rather than in Israel. All understand that it is unrealistic to expect the outcome of final status negotiations to be a full and complete return to the armistice lines of 1949. Jerusalem is and will remain the capital of Israel. The parties have agreed that Jerusalem is a matter for final status negotiations. It should remain an undivided city accessible to people of all faiths.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shimon_Peres_observes_Binyamin_Nitanyahu_greeting_Barack_Obama.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Shimon_Peres_observes_Binyamin_Nitanyahu_greeting_Barack_Obama.jpg/220px-Shimon_Peres_observes_Binyamin_Nitanyahu_greeting_Barack_Obama.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="81" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Shimon_Peres_observes_Binyamin_Nitanyahu_greeting_Barack_Obama.jpg/330px-Shimon_Peres_observes_Binyamin_Nitanyahu_greeting_Barack_Obama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Shimon_Peres_observes_Binyamin_Nitanyahu_greeting_Barack_Obama.jpg/440px-Shimon_Peres_observes_Binyamin_Nitanyahu_greeting_Barack_Obama.jpg 2x" data-file-width="976" data-file-height="360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shimon_Peres_observes_Binyamin_Nitanyahu_greeting_Barack_Obama.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Israeli Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Netanyahu" title="Benjamin Netanyahu">Benjamin Netanyahu</a> meeting with President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> at <a href="/wiki/Ben_Gurion_Airport" title="Ben Gurion Airport">Ben Gurion Airport</a> in 2013.</div></div></div>
<p>A January 2009 <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> study found that when asked "which side do you sympathize with more", 42% of Democrats and 33% of liberals (a plurality in both groups) sympathize most with the Israelis. Around half of all political moderates or independents sided with Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> The years leading up to the 2016 election have brought more discussion of the party's stance on Israel as polls reported declining support for Israel among the party faithful.<sup id="cite_ref-Galluppoll_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galluppoll-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Gallup suggested that the decline in support might be due to tensions between Israeli Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Netanyahu" title="Benjamin Netanyahu">Benjamin Netanyahu</a> and President Barack Obama.<sup id="cite_ref-Galluppoll_219-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galluppoll-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The rise of the progressive Bernie Sanders-aligned faction of the party, which tends to trend more pro-Palestine, is also likely responsible for the decline in support for Israel. A 2016 Pew Research poll found that while Clinton supporters sympathized more with Israel than Palestinians by a 20-point margin, Sanders supporters sympathized more with Palestinians than with Israel by a 6-point margin.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> In June 2016, DNC members voted against an amendment to the party platform proposed by Sanders supporter <a href="/wiki/James_Zogby" title="James Zogby">James Zogby</a> calling for an "end to occupation and illegal settlements".<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> In August 2018, <a href="/wiki/Rashida_Tlaib" title="Rashida Tlaib">Rashida Tlaib</a>, who supports a <a href="/wiki/One-state_solution" title="One-state solution">one-state solution</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ilhan_Omar" title="Ilhan Omar">Ilhan Omar</a>, who has referred to Israel as an "apartheid regime"<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> won Democratic primaries in Michigan and Minnesota. In November 2018, shortly after being elected to Congress, Omar came out in support of the <a href="/wiki/Boycott,_Divestment_and_Sanctions" title="Boycott, Divestment and Sanctions">Boycott, Divestment and Sanctions</a> (BDS) campaign against Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Voter_base">Voter base</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Factions_in_the_Democratic_Party_(United_States)#Voter_base" title="Factions in the Democratic Party (United States)">Factions in the Democratic Party (United States) §&#160;Voter base</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Republican_v_Democrat_Gallup_6-10.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Republican_v_Democrat_Gallup_6-10.svg/250px-Republican_v_Democrat_Gallup_6-10.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Republican_v_Democrat_Gallup_6-10.svg/375px-Republican_v_Democrat_Gallup_6-10.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Republican_v_Democrat_Gallup_6-10.svg/500px-Republican_v_Democrat_Gallup_6-10.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="959" data-file-height="593" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Republican_v_Democrat_Gallup_6-10.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Self-identified Democrats (<a href="/wiki/Blue_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Blue state">blue</a>) versus self-identified Republicans (<a href="/wiki/Red_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Red state">red</a>) (January–June 2010 data)</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Professionals">Professionals</span></h3>
<p>Professionals, those who have a <a href="/wiki/Higher_education" title="Higher education">college education</a> and those whose work revolves around the conception of ideas have supported the Democratic Party by a slight majority since 2000. Between 1988 and 2000, professionals favored Democrats by a 12-percentage point margin. While the professional class was once a stronghold of the Republican Party, it has become increasingly split between the two parties, leaning in favor of the Democratic Party. The increasing support for Democratic candidates among professionals may be traced to the prevalence of social liberal values among this group:<sup id="cite_ref-Judis_&amp;_Teixeira_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Judis_&amp;_Teixeira-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Professionals, who are, roughly speaking, college-educated producers of services and ideas, used to be the most staunchly Republican of all occupational groups [...] now chiefly working for large corporations and bureaucracies rather than on their own, and heavily influenced by the environmental, civil-rights, and feminist movements—began to vote Democratic. In the four elections from 1988 to 2000, they backed Democrats by an average of 52 percent to 40 percent.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite>John B. Judis and Ruy Teixeira, <i>The American Prospect</i>, June 19, 2007</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>A study on the political attitudes of <a href="/wiki/Medical_school" title="Medical school">medical students</a>, for example, found that "U.S. medical students are considerably more likely to be liberal than conservative and are more likely to be liberal than are other young U.S. adults. Future U.S. physicians may be more receptive to liberal messages than current ones, and their political orientation may profoundly affect their health system attitudes".<sup id="cite_ref-Frank,_Carrera_&amp;_Dharamsi_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frank,_Carrera_&amp;_Dharamsi-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> Similar results are found for professors, who are more strongly inclined towards liberalism and the Democratic Party than other occupational groups.<sup id="cite_ref-Kurtz,_H._(March_29,_2005)._College_Faculties_A_Most_Liberal_Lot,_Study_Finds._&#39;&#39;The_Washington_Post&#39;&#39;._106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurtz,_H._(March_29,_2005)._College_Faculties_A_Most_Liberal_Lot,_Study_Finds._&#39;&#39;The_Washington_Post&#39;&#39;.-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The Democratic Party also has strong support among <a href="/wiki/Scientist" title="Scientist">scientists</a>, with 55% identifying as Democrats, 32% as independents and 6% as Republicans and 52% identifying as liberal, 35% as moderate and 9% as conservative.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Academia">Academia</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Higher_education_in_the_United_States" title="Higher education in the United States">Higher education in the United States</a></div>
<p>Academics, intellectuals and the highly <a href="/wiki/Educational_attainment" title="Educational attainment">educated</a> overall constitute an important part of the Democratic voter base. <a href="/wiki/Academia" class="mw-redirect" title="Academia">Academia</a> in particular tends to be <a href="/wiki/Modern_liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Modern liberalism in the United States">progressive</a>. In a 2005 survey, nearly 72% of full-time faculty members identified as liberal while 15% identified as conservative. The <a href="/wiki/Social_sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Social sciences">social sciences</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a> were the most liberal disciplines while business was the most <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservative</a>. Male professors at more advanced stages of their careers as well as those at elite institutions, tend to be the most liberal.<sup id="cite_ref-Kurtz,_H._(March_29,_2005)._College_Faculties_A_Most_Liberal_Lot,_Study_Finds._&#39;&#39;The_Washington_Post&#39;&#39;._106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurtz,_H._(March_29,_2005)._College_Faculties_A_Most_Liberal_Lot,_Study_Finds._&#39;&#39;The_Washington_Post&#39;&#39;.-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Another survey by <a href="/wiki/UCLA" class="mw-redirect" title="UCLA">UCLA</a> conducted in 2001/2002 found 47.6% of scholars identifying as liberal, 34.3% as moderate and 18% as conservative.<sup id="cite_ref-Shea_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shea-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> Percentages of professors who identified as liberal ranged from 49% in business to over 80% in <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">political science</a> and the humanities.<sup id="cite_ref-Kurtz,_H._(March_29,_2005)._College_Faculties_A_Most_Liberal_Lot,_Study_Finds._&#39;&#39;The_Washington_Post&#39;&#39;._106-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurtz,_H._(March_29,_2005)._College_Faculties_A_Most_Liberal_Lot,_Study_Finds._&#39;&#39;The_Washington_Post&#39;&#39;.-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Social scientists, such as Brett O'Bannon of <a href="/wiki/DePauw_University" title="DePauw University">DePauw University</a>, have claimed that the "liberal" opinions of professors seem to have little if any, effect on the political orientation of students.<sup id="cite_ref-O&#39;Bannon,_B._R._(August_27,_2003)._In_Defense_of_the_&#39;Liberal&#39;_Professor._&#39;&#39;Indianapolis_Star&#39;&#39;._229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O&#39;Bannon,_B._R._(August_27,_2003)._In_Defense_of_the_&#39;Liberal&#39;_Professor._&#39;&#39;Indianapolis_Star&#39;&#39;.-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-George_&amp;_Medler_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-George_&amp;_Medler-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Those with <a href="/wiki/Postgraduate_education" title="Postgraduate education">graduate education</a> have become increasingly Democratic beginning in 1992,<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-exit_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-exit-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> 1996,<sup id="cite_ref-nyt-exit_231-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-exit-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> 2000,<sup id="cite_ref-CNN._(2000)._Exit_Poll._90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN._(2000)._Exit_Poll.-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> 2004<sup id="cite_ref-CNN._(2004)._Exit_Poll._91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN._(2004)._Exit_Poll.-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> and 2008<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> elections. Intellectualism, the tendency to constantly reexamine issues, or in the words of Edwards Shields, the "penetration beyond the screen of immediate concrete experience", has also been named as an explanation why academia is strongly democratic and liberal.<sup id="cite_ref-Shields_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shields-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lipset_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lipset-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the past, a self-identified Republican was more likely to have a 4-year college degree, but according to some recent surveys similar percentages of Republicans and Democrats are likely to have 4-year college degrees and Democrats are more likely to hold post-graduate degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT040112_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT040112-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An analysis of 2008 through 2012 survey data from the General Social Survey, the National Election Studies and the Pew Research Center for the People and the Press led to a slightly different assessment of the overall educational status of self-identified Democrats and Republicans:
</p>
<blockquote><p>On average, self-identified Republicans have more years of education (4 to 8 months each, depending on the survey) and are probably more likely to hold, at the least, a 4-year college degree. (One major survey indicates that they are more likely, while the results of another survey are statistically insignificant.) It also appears that Republicans continue to out-test Democrats in surveys that assess political knowledge and/or current events. With respect to post-graduate studies, the educational advantage is shifting towards self-identified Democrats. They are now more likely to hold post-graduate college degrees. (One major survey indicates that they are more likely, while the results of another survey are statistically insignificant.)<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Youth">Youth</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexandria_Ocasio-Cortez_Official_Portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Alexandria_Ocasio-Cortez_Official_Portrait.jpg/170px-Alexandria_Ocasio-Cortez_Official_Portrait.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Alexandria_Ocasio-Cortez_Official_Portrait.jpg/255px-Alexandria_Ocasio-Cortez_Official_Portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Alexandria_Ocasio-Cortez_Official_Portrait.jpg/340px-Alexandria_Ocasio-Cortez_Official_Portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3360" data-file-height="4200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexandria_Ocasio-Cortez_Official_Portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alexandria_Ocasio-Cortez" title="Alexandria Ocasio-Cortez">Alexandria Ocasio-Cortez</a> is the youngest woman ever elected to the House of Representatives, at 29 years old.</div></div></div>
<p>Studies have shown that younger voters tend to vote mostly for Democratic candidates in recent years. Despite supporting <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>, the young have voted in favor of the Democratic presidential candidate in every election since <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> in 1992 and are more likely to identify as liberals than the general population.<sup id="cite_ref-Nagourney_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nagourney-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2004_United_States_presidential_election" title="2004 United States presidential election">2004 presidential election</a>, Democratic presidential candidate <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a> received 54% of the vote from voters of the age group 18–29 while Republican <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> received 45% of the vote from the same age group. In the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Midterm_election" title="Midterm election">midterm elections</a>, the Democrats received 60% of the vote from the same age group.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN._(2004)._Exit_Poll._91-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN._(2004)._Exit_Poll.-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN._(2006)._Exit_Poll._92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN._(2006)._Exit_Poll.-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Polls suggest that younger voters tend to be more liberal than the general population and have more liberal views than the public on same-sex marriage and universal healthcare, helping <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> carry 66% of their votes in 2008. The <a href="/wiki/Young_Democrats_of_America" title="Young Democrats of America">Young Democrats of America</a> are an affiliated organization of members of the party younger than 36 that advocates for youth issues and works for youth voter turnout.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women">Women</span></h3>
<p>Although the gender gap has varied over many years, women of all ages are more likely than men to identify as Democrats. Polls in 2009 indicated that 41% of women identify as Democrats while only 25% of women identify as Republicans and 26% as independents whereas 32% of men identify as Democrats, 28% as Republicans and 34% as independents. Among ethnic minorities, women also are more likely than males to identify as Democrats. Also, American women that identified as single, living with a domestic partner, divorced, separated, or widowed are more likely than men in these categories to vote Democratic, in contrast to married Americans which split about equally between Democrat and Republican. Women in these categories are significantly more likely than males in these categories to vote Democratic.<sup id="cite_ref-gallup2010_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gallup2010-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Federation_of_Democratic_Women" title="National Federation of Democratic Women">National Federation of Democratic Women</a> is an affiliated organization meant to advocate for women's issues. The <a href="/wiki/Woman%27s_National_Democratic_Club" title="Woman&#39;s National Democratic Club">Woman's National Democratic Club</a> works to promote the Democratic party. National women's organizations that support Democratic candidates are <a href="/wiki/Emily%27s_List" class="mw-redirect" title="Emily&#39;s List">Emily's List</a>, which aims to help elect pro-choice Democratic female candidates to office and often but not always the <a href="/wiki/National_Organization_for_Women" title="National Organization for Women">National Organization for Women</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_marital_status_and_parenthood">Relation to marital status and parenthood</span></h3>
<p>Americans that identify as single, living with a domestic partner, divorced, separated, or widowed are more likely to vote Democratic in contrast to married Americans which split about equally between Democrat and Republican.<sup id="cite_ref-gallup2010_238-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gallup2010-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/General_Social_Survey" title="General Social Survey">GSS</a> surveys of more than 11,000 Democrats and Republicans conducted between 1996 and 2006 came to the result that the differences in <a href="/wiki/Fertility_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertility rate">fertility rates</a> are not statistically significant between these parties, with the average Democrat having 1.94 children and the average Republican having 1.91 children.<sup id="cite_ref-Fried2008_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fried2008-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> However, there is a significant difference in fertility rates between the two related groups <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Liberalism in the United States">liberals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservatives</a>, with liberals reproducing at a much lower rate than conservatives.<sup id="cite_ref-Fried2008_239-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fried2008-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="LGBTQ_Americans">LGBTQ Americans</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gay_Rights_are_Human_Rights_(5823033786).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Gay_Rights_are_Human_Rights_%285823033786%29.jpg/220px-Gay_Rights_are_Human_Rights_%285823033786%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Gay_Rights_are_Human_Rights_%285823033786%29.jpg/330px-Gay_Rights_are_Human_Rights_%285823033786%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Gay_Rights_are_Human_Rights_%285823033786%29.jpg/440px-Gay_Rights_are_Human_Rights_%285823033786%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3093" data-file-height="2062" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gay_Rights_are_Human_Rights_(5823033786).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Gay Rights are Human Rights", a quote by Democratic Secretary of State and Senator from <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a></div></div></div>
