<p>According to national media exit polling, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_Americans" class="mw-redirect" title="LGBT Americans">LGBT Americans</a> typically vote Democratic in national elections within the 70–77% range. In heavily gay precincts in large cities across the nation, the average was higher, ranging from 85–94%. This trend has continued since 1996 when Bill Clinton won 71% of the LGBT vote compared to Bob Dole's 16% and 13% for others. In 2000, Al Gore won 70% to George W. Bush's 25% with 5% for others, in 2004 John Kerry won 77% to George W. Bush's 23%, in 2008 Barack Obama won 70% to John McCain's 27% with 3% to others and in 2012 Barack Obama won 76% to Mitt Romney's 22% with 2% to others. Patrick Egan, a professor of politics at New York University specializing in LGBT voting patterns, calls this a "remarkable continuity", saying that "about three-fourths vote Democratic and one-fourth Republican from year to year".<sup id="cite_ref-press_240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-press-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Notable LGBT Democrats include current Senator <a href="/wiki/Tammy_Baldwin" title="Tammy Baldwin">Tammy Baldwin</a> of Wisconsin, current Representative <a href="/wiki/David_Cicilline" title="David Cicilline">David Cicilline</a> of Rhode Island, and current Governor <a href="/wiki/Jared_Polis" title="Jared Polis">Jared Polis</a> of Colorado. The late activist and San Francisco Supervisor <a href="/wiki/Harvey_Milk" title="Harvey Milk">Harvey Milk</a> was a Democrat as is former Representative <a href="/wiki/Barney_Frank" title="Barney Frank">Barney Frank</a> of Massachusetts. The <a href="/wiki/National_Stonewall_Democrats" class="mw-redirect" title="National Stonewall Democrats">National Stonewall Democrats</a> is an LGBT advocacy group associated with the Democratic Party. The <a href="/wiki/Congressional_LGBT_Equality_Caucus" title="Congressional LGBT Equality Caucus">Congressional LGBT Equality Caucus</a> is a congressional caucus of 153 Democrats that work and advocate for LGBT rights within the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lgbtcaucus_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lgbtcaucus-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor">Labor</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:372px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fig_109_-_does_someone_in_house_belong_to_union.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Fig_109_-_does_someone_in_house_belong_to_union.JPG/370px-Fig_109_-_does_someone_in_house_belong_to_union.JPG" decoding="async" width="370" height="296" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/19/Fig_109_-_does_someone_in_house_belong_to_union.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="525" data-file-height="420" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fig_109_-_does_someone_in_house_belong_to_union.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Higher percentuals of household who belongs to unions are more likely to be Democrats than Republicans</div></div></div>
<p>Since the 1930s, a critical component of the Democratic Party coalition has been <a href="/wiki/Labor_unions_in_the_United_States" title="Labor unions in the United States">organized labor</a>. Labor unions supply a great deal of the money, <a href="/wiki/Grassroots" title="Grassroots">grass roots</a> <a href="/wiki/Political_organisation" title="Political organisation">political organization</a> and voting base of support for the party. Democrats are far more likely to be represented by unions, although union membership has declined, in general, during the last few decades. This trend is depicted in the following graph from the book <i>Democrats and Republicans—Rhetoric and Reality</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> It is based on surveys conducted by the National Election Studies (NES).
</p><p>The historic decline in union membership over the past half-century has been accompanied by a growing disparity between public sector and private sector union membership percentages. The three most significant labor groupings in the Democratic coalition today are the <a href="/wiki/AFL-CIO" class="mw-redirect" title="AFL-CIO">AFL-CIO</a> and <a href="/wiki/Change_to_Win_Federation" title="Change to Win Federation">Change to Win</a> <a href="/wiki/National_trade_union_center" title="National trade union center">labor federations</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/National_Education_Association" title="National Education Association">National Education Association</a>, a large, unaffiliated <a href="/wiki/Teacher" title="Teacher">teachers'</a> union. Both the AFL-CIO and Change to Win have identified their top legislative priority for 2007 as passage of the <a href="/wiki/Employee_Free_Choice_Act" title="Employee Free Choice Act">Employee Free Choice Act</a>. Other important issues for labor unions include supporting <a href="/wiki/Industrial_policy" title="Industrial policy">industrial policy</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionism</a>) that sustains unionized <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">manufacturing</a> jobs, raising the <a href="/wiki/Minimum_wage" title="Minimum wage">minimum wage</a> and promoting broad social programs such as <a href="/wiki/Social_Security_(United_States)" title="Social Security (United States)">Social Security</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal health care</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Working_class">Working class</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Social_class_in_the_United_States" title="Social class in the United States">Social class in the United States</a></div>
<p>While the <a href="/wiki/American_working_class" class="mw-redirect" title="American working class">American working class</a> has lost much of its political strength with the decline of <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">labor unions</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-What&#39;s_Class_Got_To_Do_With_It,_American_Society_in_the_Twenty-First_Century_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-What&#39;s_Class_Got_To_Do_With_It,_American_Society_in_the_Twenty-First_Century-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> it remains a stronghold of the Democratic Party and continues as an essential part of the Democratic base. Today, roughly a third of the American public is estimated to be working class with around 52% being either members of the working or <a href="/wiki/American_lower_class" title="American lower class">lower classes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_American_Class_Structure_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_American_Class_Structure-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Society_in_Focus_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Society_in_Focus-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> Yet as those with lower <a href="/wiki/Social_class_in_the_United_States" title="Social class in the United States">socioeconomic status</a> are less likely to vote, the working and lower classes are underrepresented in the electorate. The working class is largely distinguished by highly routinized and closely supervised work. It consists mainly of <a href="/wiki/Clerk_(position)#United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Clerk (position)">clerical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blue-collar_worker" title="Blue-collar worker">blue-collar workers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_American_Class_Structure_245-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_American_Class_Structure-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> Even though most in the working class are able to afford an adequate <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a>, high economic insecurity and possible personal benefit from an extended <a href="/wiki/Social_safety_net" title="Social safety net">social safety net</a> make the majority of working class person left-of-center on economic issues. However, most working class Democrats differ from most liberals in their more socially conservative views. Working class Democrats tend to be more religious and likely to belong to an ethnic minority. Socially conservative and disadvantaged Democrats are among the least educated and lowest earning ideological demographics. In 2005, only 15% had a college degree, compared to 27% at the national average and 49% of liberals, respectively. Together, socially conservative and the financially disadvantaged comprised roughly 54% of the Democratic base.<sup id="cite_ref-Pew_Research_Center._105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pew_Research_Center.-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The continued importance of the working class votes manifests itself in recent CNN exit polls, which shows that the majority of those with low <a href="/wiki/Income_in_the_United_States" title="Income in the United States">incomes</a> and little education vote for the Democratic Party.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN._(2000)._Exit_Poll._90-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN._(2000)._Exit_Poll.-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN._(2004)._Exit_Poll._91-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN._(2004)._Exit_Poll.-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN._(2006)._Exit_Poll._92-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN._(2006)._Exit_Poll.-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, <a href="/wiki/Reagan_Democrat" title="Reagan Democrat">since at least 1980</a><sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> there has been a noticeable decline in support for the Democratic Party among white working-class voters.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> In the 2008 presidential election, Barack Obama carried 40% of white voters without college degrees to John McCain carrying 58%.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> In the 2010 midterms, Democratic candidates for the House of Representatives only carried 33% of the white working class vote compared to 63% for the Republican candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> In the 2012 presidential election, Barack Obama carried 36% of white working class voters to Mitt Romney carrying 61%.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> In the 2014 midterms, Democratic candidates for the House of Representatives carried 34% of the white working class vote compared to 64% for the Republican candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> In the 2016 presidential election, Hillary Clinton only carried 28% of white working class voters to Donald Trump carrying 67%.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secular_Americans">Secular Americans</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Irreligion_in_the_United_States" title="Irreligion in the United States">Irreligion in the United States</a></div>
<p>The Democratic Party receives support from secular organizations such as the <a href="/wiki/Secular_Coalition_for_America" title="Secular Coalition for America">Secular Coalition for America</a><sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> and many <a href="/wiki/Agnostic" class="mw-redirect" title="Agnostic">agnostic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atheist" class="mw-redirect" title="Atheist">atheist</a> Americans. Exit polls from the 2008 election showed that voters with a religious affiliation of "<a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">none</a>" accounted for the 12% of the electorate and overwhelmingly voted for Obama by a 75–25% margin.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> In his inaugural address, Obama acknowledged atheists by saying that the United States is not just "Christians and Muslims, Jews and Hindus but non-believers as well".<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2012_United_States_presidential_election" title="2012 United States presidential election">2012 election cycle</a>, Obama has moderate to high rankings with the Secular Coalition for America whereas the majority of the Republican candidates have ratings in the low-to-failing range.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although a diverse group themselves, atheists and secular people may include individuals who are <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_conservatism" title="Fiscal conservatism">fiscally conservative</a>. In this case, fiscally conservative atheists and secularists will come together due to their opposition to the religiously bound social policies of the Christian right.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> There is still a <a href="/wiki/Social_stigma" title="Social stigma">social stigma</a> relating to <a href="/wiki/Irreligion_in_the_United_States" title="Irreligion in the United States">atheism in the nation</a> and polls show that a majority of the American people would be more comfortable voting for a Muslim or gay candidate than an atheist.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="African_Americans">African Americans</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Photograph_of_President_William_Jefferson_Clinton_Greeting_People_in_a_Large_Crowd_at_a_%22Get_Out_the_Vote%22_Rally_in_Los_Angeles,_California,_11_02_2000.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Photograph_of_President_William_Jefferson_Clinton_Greeting_People_in_a_Large_Crowd_at_a_%22Get_Out_the_Vote%22_Rally_in_Los_Angeles%2C_California%2C_11_02_2000.jpg/220px-Photograph_of_President_William_Jefferson_Clinton_Greeting_People_in_a_Large_Crowd_at_a_%22Get_Out_the_Vote%22_Rally_in_Los_Angeles%2C_California%2C_11_02_2000.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Photograph_of_President_William_Jefferson_Clinton_Greeting_People_in_a_Large_Crowd_at_a_%22Get_Out_the_Vote%22_Rally_in_Los_Angeles%2C_California%2C_11_02_2000.jpg/330px-Photograph_of_President_William_Jefferson_Clinton_Greeting_People_in_a_Large_Crowd_at_a_%22Get_Out_the_Vote%22_Rally_in_Los_Angeles%2C_California%2C_11_02_2000.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Photograph_of_President_William_Jefferson_Clinton_Greeting_People_in_a_Large_Crowd_at_a_%22Get_Out_the_Vote%22_Rally_in_Los_Angeles%2C_California%2C_11_02_2000.jpg/440px-Photograph_of_President_William_Jefferson_Clinton_Greeting_People_in_a_Large_Crowd_at_a_%22Get_Out_the_Vote%22_Rally_in_Los_Angeles%2C_California%2C_11_02_2000.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3150" data-file-height="2026" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Photograph_of_President_William_Jefferson_Clinton_Greeting_People_in_a_Large_Crowd_at_a_%22Get_Out_the_Vote%22_Rally_in_Los_Angeles,_California,_11_02_2000.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bill Clinton at a Democratic "<a href="/wiki/Get_out_the_vote" title="Get out the vote">Get out the vote</a>" rally in Los Angeles</div></div></div>
<p>From the end of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a> primarily favored the Republican Party due to its overwhelming political and more tangible efforts in achieving the abolition of <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">slavery</a>, particularly through <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">President Lincoln</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">Emancipation Proclamation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Blacks_and_the_Democratic_Party_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blacks_and_the_Democratic_Party-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> The South had long been a Democratic stronghold, favoring a state's right to legal slavery. In addition, the ranks of the fledgling <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a> were composed almost entirely of white Democrats who were angry over the poor treatment they had received at the hands of northerners and who were also bent on reversing the policies of <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Reconstruction Era of the United States">Reconstruction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> However, African Americans began drifting to the Democratic Party when <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> was elected president.<sup id="cite_ref-Blacks_and_the_Democratic_Party_267-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blacks_and_the_Democratic_Party-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> Support for the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil Rights Movement">Civil Rights Movement</a> in the 1960s by Democratic Presidents <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> helped give the Democrats even larger support among the African American community, which consistently vote between 85-95% Democratic.<sup id="cite_ref-Blacks_and_the_Democratic_Party_267-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blacks_and_the_Democratic_Party-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Prominent modern-day African American Democratic politicians include <a href="/wiki/Jim_Clyburn" title="Jim Clyburn">Jim Clyburn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elijah_Cummings" title="Elijah Cummings">Elijah Cummings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maxine_Waters" title="Maxine Waters">Maxine Waters</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Lewis_(Georgia)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Lewis (Georgia)">John Lewis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Lee" title="Barbara Lee">Barbara Lee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Rangel" title="Charles Rangel">Charles Rangel</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Conyers" title="John Conyers">John Conyers</a>, Senator <a href="/wiki/Cory_Booker" title="Cory Booker">Cory Booker</a> and former President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>, who managed to net over 95% of the African American vote in the 2008 election.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> Despite being unaffiliated, the <a href="/wiki/NAACP" title="NAACP">NAACP</a> often participates in organizing and voter turnout drives and advocates for progressive causes, especially those that affect people of color.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> Within the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Black_Caucus" title="Congressional Black Caucus">Congressional Black Caucus</a>, consisting of 44 black Democrats, serves to represent the interests of African Americans and advocate on issues that affect them.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans">Hispanic and Latino Americans</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Juli%C3%A1n_Castro%27s_Official_HUD_Portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Juli%C3%A1n_Castro%27s_Official_HUD_Portrait.jpg/150px-Juli%C3%A1n_Castro%27s_Official_HUD_Portrait.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Juli%C3%A1n_Castro%27s_Official_HUD_Portrait.jpg/225px-Juli%C3%A1n_Castro%27s_Official_HUD_Portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Juli%C3%A1n_Castro%27s_Official_HUD_Portrait.jpg/300px-Juli%C3%A1n_Castro%27s_Official_HUD_Portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2675" data-file-height="3820" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Juli%C3%A1n_Castro%27s_Official_HUD_Portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Julian_Castro" title="Julian Castro">Julián Castro</a> served as <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_San_Antonio" class="mw-redirect" title="Mayor of San Antonio">Mayor</a> of <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio" title="San Antonio">San Antonio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_Housing_and_Urban_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of Housing and Urban Development">Secretary</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Housing_and_Urban_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="Department of Housing and Urban Development">Department of Housing and Urban Development</a> and is a candidate for President in <a href="/wiki/2020_United_States_Presidential_election" class="mw-redirect" title="2020 United States Presidential election">2020</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_and_Latino_Americans" title="Hispanic and Latino Americans">Hispanic</a> population, particularly the large <a href="/wiki/Mexican_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexican American">Mexican American</a> population in the <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States" title="Southwestern United States">Southwest</a> and the large <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Ricans_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Puerto Ricans in the United States">Puerto Rican</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dominican_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Dominican American">Dominican</a> populations in the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeast</a>, have been strong supporters of the Democratic Party. In the <a href="/wiki/1996_United_States_presidential_election" title="1996 United States presidential election">1996 presidential election</a>, Democratic President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> received 72% of the Hispanic vote.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> In following years, the Republican Party gained increasing support from the Hispanic community, especially among Hispanic Protestants and <a href="/wiki/Pentecostalism" title="Pentecostalism">Pentecostals</a>. With his much more liberal views on immigration, President Bush was the first Republican president to gain 40% of the Hispanic vote in the <a href="/wiki/2004_United_States_presidential_election" title="2004 United States presidential election">2004 presidential election</a>. Yet the Republican Party's support among Hispanics eroded in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_2006" class="mw-redirect" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 2006">2006 midterm elections</a>, dropping from 44% to 30%, with the Democrats gaining in the Hispanic vote from 55% in 2004 to 69% in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN._(2004)._Exit_Poll._91-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN._(2004)._Exit_Poll.-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN._(2006)._Exit_Poll._92-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN._(2006)._Exit_Poll.-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Democrats increased their share of the Hispanic vote in the <a href="/wiki/2008_United_States_presidential_election" title="2008 United States presidential election">2008 presidential election</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> receiving 67%. According to exit polls by Edison Research, Obama increased his support again in 2012, winning 71% of Hispanic voters.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cuban_Americans" title="Cuban Americans">Cuban Americans</a> still tend to vote Republican, though there has been a noticeable change starting with the 2008 elections. During the 2008 elections, Barack Obama received 47% of the Cuban American vote in Florida.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> According to Bendixen's <a href="/wiki/Exit_polls" class="mw-redirect" title="Exit polls">exit polls</a>, 84% of Miami-Dade Cuban American voters 65 or older backed McCain, while 55% of those 29 or younger backed Obama,<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> showing that the younger Cuban American generation has become more liberal.
</p><p>Throughout the decade of the 2000s, 60% or more of Hispanic <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholics" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholics">Roman Catholics</a> who were registered to vote identified as either Democratic or leaning towards the Party.<sup id="cite_ref-growing_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-growing-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unaffiliated Hispanic advocacy groups that often support progressive candidates and causes include the <a href="/wiki/National_Council_of_La_Raza" class="mw-redirect" title="National Council of La Raza">National Council of La Raza</a> and the <a href="/wiki/League_of_United_Latin_American_Citizens" title="League of United Latin American Citizens">League of United Latin American Citizens</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a>, the Democratic caucus of Hispanic Americans is the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Hispanic_Caucus" title="Congressional Hispanic Caucus">Congressional Hispanic Caucus</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Native_Americans">Native Americans</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:161px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CarlVenneANDBarackObama-May19-2008.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/30/CarlVenneANDBarackObama-May19-2008.JPG" decoding="async" width="159" height="172" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="159" data-file-height="172" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CarlVenneANDBarackObama-May19-2008.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Carl_Venne" title="Carl Venne">Carl Venne</a>, the then <a href="/wiki/Chairpersons_of_the_Crow_Nation" title="Chairpersons of the Crow Nation">Crow Indian Tribal Chairman</a>, shows support for Democratic presidential nominee Barack Obama in 2008.</div></div></div>
<p>The Democratic Party also has strong support among the <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> population, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington_(U.S._state)" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington (U.S. state)">Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a><sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>. Although now a small percentage of the population (virtually non-existent in some regions), most Native American precincts vote Democratic in margins exceeded only by African-Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern-day Democratic Native American politicians include Congresswomen <a href="/wiki/Sharice_Davids" title="Sharice Davids">Sharice Davids</a> of Kansas and <a href="/wiki/Deb_Haaland" title="Deb Haaland">Deb Haaland</a> of New Mexico and former Congressman <a href="/wiki/Brad_Carson" title="Brad Carson">Brad Carson</a> of Oklahoma as well as Principal Chief <a href="/wiki/Bill_John_Baker" title="Bill John Baker">Bill John Baker</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Cherokee_Nation" title="Cherokee Nation">Cherokee Nation</a> and Governor <a href="/wiki/Bill_Anoatubby" title="Bill Anoatubby">Bill Anoatubby</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Chickasaw_Nation" title="Chickasaw Nation">Chickasaw Nation</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish_Americans">Jewish Americans</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/National_Jewish_Democratic_Council" title="National Jewish Democratic Council">National Jewish Democratic Council</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Obama_and_Conference_of_Presidents_of_Major_American_Jewish_Organizations.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Obama_and_Conference_of_Presidents_of_Major_American_Jewish_Organizations.jpg/200px-Obama_and_Conference_of_Presidents_of_Major_American_Jewish_Organizations.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Obama_and_Conference_of_Presidents_of_Major_American_Jewish_Organizations.jpg/300px-Obama_and_Conference_of_Presidents_of_Major_American_Jewish_Organizations.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Obama_and_Conference_of_Presidents_of_Major_American_Jewish_Organizations.jpg/400px-Obama_and_Conference_of_Presidents_of_Major_American_Jewish_Organizations.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4096" data-file-height="2730" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Obama_and_Conference_of_Presidents_of_Major_American_Jewish_Organizations.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Democratic President Barack Obama at a <a href="/wiki/Conference_of_Presidents_of_Major_American_Jewish_Organizations" title="Conference of Presidents of Major American Jewish Organizations">Conference of Presidents of Major American Jewish Organizations</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/American_Jews" title="American Jews">Jewish American</a> communities tend to be a stronghold for the Democratic Party, with more than 70% of Jewish voters having cast their ballots for the Democrats in the 2004 and 2006 elections.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN._(2004)._Exit_Poll._91-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN._(2004)._Exit_Poll.-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN._(2006)._Exit_Poll._92-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN._(2006)._Exit_Poll.-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a> received 79% of the Jewish votes in 2000 and Barack Obama won about 77% of the Jewish vote in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> Support tends to vary among specific sectarian groups. For example, only 13% of <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="Orthodox Jews">Orthodox Jews</a> supported Barack Obama in 2008 while around 60% of <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="Conservative Jews">Conservative Jews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reform_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="Reform Jews">Reform Jews</a> did so.<sup id="cite_ref-ajc_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ajc-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> A 2010 poll by the Pew Research Center found that 60% of self-described Jews identified as Democratic or leaning towards the party, compared to 33% with those feelings towards Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-growing_275-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-growing-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Jews as an important Democratic constituency are especially politically active and influential in large cities such as <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> and play critical roles in large cities within presidential <a href="/wiki/Swing_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Swing states">swing states</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">Miami</a> and <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas_Valley" title="Las Vegas Valley">Las Vegas</a>. Many prominent national Democrats in recent decades have been Jewish, including <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Schumer" title="Chuck Schumer">Chuck Schumer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Levin" title="Carl Levin">Carl Levin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Ribicoff" title="Abraham Ribicoff">Abraham Ribicoff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ben_Cardin" title="Ben Cardin">Ben Cardin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Waxman" title="Henry Waxman">Henry Waxman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Frost" title="Martin Frost">Martin Frost</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lieberman" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Lieberman">Joseph Lieberman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Sanders" title="Bernie Sanders">Bernie Sanders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dianne_Feinstein" title="Dianne Feinstein">Dianne Feinstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barney_Frank" title="Barney Frank">Barney Frank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Boxer" title="Barbara Boxer">Barbara Boxer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Wellstone" title="Paul Wellstone">Paul Wellstone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rahm_Emanuel" title="Rahm Emanuel">Rahm Emanuel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russ_Feingold" title="Russ Feingold">Russ Feingold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herb_Kohl" title="Herb Kohl">Herb Kohl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Howard_Metzenbaum" title="Howard Metzenbaum">Howard Metzenbaum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ajc_279-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ajc-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_and_Muslim_Americans">Arab and Muslim Americans</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arab_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab American">Arab Americans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_the_United_States" title="Islam in the United States">Muslim Americans</a> have leaned Democratic since the <a href="/wiki/2003_invasion_of_Iraq" title="2003 invasion of Iraq">Iraq War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-VOA_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VOA-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zogby_International" class="mw-redirect" title="Zogby International">Zogby</a> found in June 2007 that 39% of Arab Americans identify as Democrats, 26% as Republicans and 28% as <a href="/wiki/Independent_(voter)" class="mw-redirect" title="Independent (voter)">independents</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-VOA_280-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VOA-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Arab Americans, who are in general socially conservative yet with more diverse economic views, historically voted Republican until recent years, having supported <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> over <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a> in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2012 poll found that 68% of Muslim Americans surveyed support Barack Obama.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asian_Americans">Asian Americans</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Daniel_Inouye,_official_Senate_photo_portrait,_2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Daniel_Inouye%2C_official_Senate_photo_portrait%2C_2008.jpg/150px-Daniel_Inouye%2C_official_Senate_photo_portrait%2C_2008.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Daniel_Inouye%2C_official_Senate_photo_portrait%2C_2008.jpg/225px-Daniel_Inouye%2C_official_Senate_photo_portrait%2C_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Daniel_Inouye%2C_official_Senate_photo_portrait%2C_2008.jpg/300px-Daniel_Inouye%2C_official_Senate_photo_portrait%2C_2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="854" data-file-height="1067" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Daniel_Inouye,_official_Senate_photo_portrait,_2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Daniel_Inouye" title="Daniel Inouye">Daniel Inouye</a> was a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Medal_of_Honor" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Medal of Honor">Medal of Honor</a> recipient who served nearly 60 years in elected office as a Democrat. He was the first <a href="/wiki/Japanese-American" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese-American">Japanese-American</a> elected to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Democratic Party also has considerable support in the small yet growing <a href="/wiki/Asian_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Asian American">Asian American</a> population. The Asian American population had been a stronghold of the Republican Party until the 1992 presidential election in which <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a> won 55% of the Asian American vote, compared to Bill Clinton winning 31% and <a href="/wiki/Ross_Perot" title="Ross Perot">Ross Perot</a> winning 15% of the Asian vote. Originally, the vast majority of Asian Americans consisted of strongly anti-communist, pro-democracy <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Vietnamese American">Vietnamese refugees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Americans" title="Chinese Americans">Chinese Americans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Americans" title="Taiwanese Americans">Taiwanese Americans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_Americans" title="Korean Americans">Korean Americans</a> and socially conservative <a href="/wiki/Filipino_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Filipino American">Filipinos</a> who fled <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Marcos" title="Ferdinand Marcos">Ferdinand Marcos</a> in the 1960s through the 1980s and the general <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a>'s socially conservative, fervently anti-communist position strongly resonated with this original demographic. The Democratic party made gains among the Asian American population starting in 1996 and in 2006, won 62% of the Asian American vote. Exit polls after the 2008 presidential election indicated that Democratic candidate, <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> won 62% of the Asian American vote nationwide.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> In the 2012 presidential election, 73% of the Asian American electorate voted for Obama's re-election.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Barack Obama has the support of 85% of Indian Americans, 68% of Chinese Americans and 57% of Filipino Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> The Asian American community's increasing number of young voters has also helped to erode traditionally reliably Republican voting blocs such as Vietnamese and Filipino Americans, leading to an increase in support for Democrats. Prominent Asian American Democrats include Senators <a href="/wiki/Tammy_Duckworth" title="Tammy Duckworth">Tammy Duckworth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Inouye" title="Daniel Inouye">Daniel Inouye</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Akaka" title="Daniel Akaka">Daniel Akaka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mazie_Hirono" title="Mazie Hirono">Mazie Hirono</a>, former Governor and Secretary of Commerce <a href="/wiki/Gary_Locke" title="Gary Locke">Gary Locke</a> and Representatives <a href="/wiki/Mike_Honda" title="Mike Honda">Mike Honda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judy_Chu" title="Judy Chu">Judy Chu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doris_Matsui" title="Doris Matsui">Doris Matsui</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norman_Mineta" title="Norman Mineta">Norman Mineta</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Democratic_presidents">Democratic presidents</span></h2>

</p>

<td>33
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/File:Truman_58-766-09.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Truman 58-766-09.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Truman_58-766-09.jpg/65px-Truman_58-766-09.jpg" decoding="async" width="65" height="91" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Truman_58-766-09.jpg/98px-Truman_58-766-09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Truman_58-766-09.jpg/130px-Truman_58-766-09.jpg 2x" data-file-width="279" data-file-height="391" /></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Supreme_Court_justices_appointed_by_Democratic_presidents">Supreme Court justices appointed by Democratic presidents</span></h2>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Supreme Court">Supreme Court</a>, as of May&#160;2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Democratic_Party_(United_States)&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, four of the nine seats are filled by justices appointed by Democratic presidents.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-died-286"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-died_286-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-died_286-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Died in office.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: upper-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-290"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-290">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although Tilden won a majority of the popular vote, Republican <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_B._Hayes" title="Rutherford B. Hayes">Rutherford B. Hayes</a> won a majority of votes in the Electoral College.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-291"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-291">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although Cleveland won a majority of the popular vote, Republican <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Harrison" title="Benjamin Harrison">Benjamin Harrison</a> won a majority of votes in the Electoral College.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-292"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-292">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although Gore won a majority of the popular vote, Republican <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> won a majority of votes in the Electoral College.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-293"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-293">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although Clinton won a majority of the popular vote, Republican <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> won a majority of votes in the Electoral College.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-Regional_Variations_in_the_Realignment_of_American_Politics,_1944–2004-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Regional_Variations_in_the_Realignment_of_American_Politics,_1944–2004_57-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Bullock, Charles S.; Hoffman, Donna R.; Gaddie, Ronald Keith (2006). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://doi.wiley.com/10.1111/j.1540-6237.2006.00393.x">"Regional Variations in the Realignment of American Politics, 1944–2004"</a>. <i>Social Science Quarterly</i>. <b>87</b> (3): 494–518. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1111%2Fj.1540-6237.2006.00393.x">10.1111/j.1540-6237.2006.00393.x</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0038-4941">0038-4941</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 9,</span> 2018</span>. <q>The events of 1964 laid open the divisions between the South and national Democrats and elicited distinctly different voter behavior in the two regions. The agitation for civil rights by southern blacks, continued white violence toward the civil rights movement, and President Lyndon Johnson's aggressive leadership all facilitated passage of the 1964 Civil Rights Act. [...] In the South, 1964 should be associated with GOP growth while in the Northeast this election contributed to the eradication of Republicans.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Social+Science+Quarterly&amp;rft.atitle=Regional+Variations+in+the+Realignment+of+American+Politics%2C+1944%E2%80%932004&amp;rft.volume=87&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=494-518&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1111%2Fj.1540-6237.2006.00393.x&amp;rft.issn=0038-4941&amp;rft.aulast=Bullock&amp;rft.aufirst=Charles+S.&amp;rft.au=Hoffman%2C+Donna+R.&amp;rft.au=Gaddie%2C+Ronald+Keith&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fdoi.wiley.com%2F10.1111%2Fj.1540-6237.2006.00393.x&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADemocratic+Party+%28United+States%29" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Gaddie, Ronald Keith (February 17, 2012). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://oxfordhandbooks.com/view/10.1093/oxfordhb/9780195381948.001.0001/oxfordhb-9780195381948-e-13">"Realignment"</a>. <i>Oxford Handbooks Online</i>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1093%2Foxfordhb%2F9780195381948.013.0013">10.1093/oxfordhb/9780195381948.013.0013</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Stanley, Harold W. (1988). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.journals.uchicago.edu/doi/10.2307/2131041">"Southern Partisan Changes: Dealignment, Realignment or Both?"</a>. <i>The Journal of Politics</i>. <b>50</b> (1): 64–88. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.2307%2F2131041">10.2307/2131041</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0022-3816">0022-3816</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 9,</span> 2018</span>. <q>Events surrounding the presidential election of 1964 marked a watershed in terms of the parties and the South (Pomper, 1972). The Solid South was built around the identification of the Democratic party with the cause of white supremacy. Events before 1964 gave white southerners pause about the linkage between the Democratic party and white supremacy, but the 1964 election, passage of the Civil Rights Act of 1964, and the Voting Rights Act of 1965 altered in the minds of most the positions of the national parties on racial issues.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Journal+of+Politics&amp;rft.atitle=Southern+Partisan+Changes%3A+Dealignment%2C+Realignment+or+Both%3F&amp;rft.volume=50&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=64-88&amp;rft.date=1988&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2307%2F2131041&amp;rft.issn=0022-3816&amp;rft.aulast=Stanley&amp;rft.aufirst=Harold+W.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.journals.uchicago.edu%2Fdoi%2F10.2307%2F2131041&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADemocratic+Party+%28United+States%29" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Miller, Gary; Schofield, Norman (2008). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/perspectives-on-politics/article/transformation-of-the-republican-and-democratic-party-coalitions-in-the-us/B7CC676C1D75A061F39FA8345D036BC0">"The Transformation of the Republican and Democratic Party Coalitions in the U.S."</a> <i>Perspectives on Politics</i>. <b>6</b> (3): 433–50. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1017%2FS1537592708081218">10.1017/S1537592708081218</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/1541-0986">1541-0986</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 9,</span> 2018</span>. <q>1964 was the last presidential election in which the Democrats earned more than 50 percent of the white vote in the United States.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Perspectives+on+Politics&amp;rft.atitle=The+Transformation+of+the+Republican+and+Democratic+Party+Coalitions+in+the+U.S.&amp;rft.volume=6&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=433-50&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1017%2FS1537592708081218&amp;rft.issn=1541-0986&amp;rft.aulast=Miller&amp;rft.aufirst=Gary&amp;rft.au=Schofield%2C+Norman&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cambridge.org%2Fcore%2Fjournals%2Fperspectives-on-politics%2Farticle%2Ftransformation-of-the-republican-and-democratic-party-coalitions-in-the-us%2FB7CC676C1D75A061F39FA8345D036BC0&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADemocratic+Party+%28United+States%29" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.hup.harvard.edu/catalog.php?isbn=9780674012486">"The Rise of Southern Republicans – Earl Black, Merle Black"</a>. <i>hup.harvard.edu</i>. Harvard University Press<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 9,</span> 2018</span>. <q>When the Republican party nominated Arizona Senator Barry Goldwater—one of the few northern senators who had opposed the Civil Rights Act—as their presidential candidate in 1964, the party attracted many racist southern whites but permanently alienated African-American voters. Beginning with the Goldwater-versus-Johnson campaign more southern whites voted Republican than Democratic, a pattern that has recurred in every subsequent presidential election. [...] Before the 1964 presidential election the Republican party had not carried any Deep South state for eighty-eight years. Yet shortly after Congress passed the Civil Rights Act, hundreds of Deep South counties gave Barry Goldwater landslide majorities.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=hup.harvard.edu&amp;rft.atitle=The+Rise+of+Southern+Republicans+%E2%80%93+Earl+Black%2C+Merle+Black&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.hup.harvard.edu%2Fcatalog.php%3Fisbn%3D9780674012486&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADemocratic+Party+%28United+States%29" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Issue_Evolution-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Issue_Evolution_62-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Issue_Evolution_62-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://press.princeton.edu/titles/4385.html">"Issue Evolution"</a>. Princeton University Press<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 9,</span> 2018</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Issue+Evolution&amp;rft.pub=Princeton+University+Press&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fpress.princeton.edu%2Ftitles%2F4385.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADemocratic+Party+%28United+States%29" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Miller, Gary; Schofield, Norman (2003). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.cambridge.org/core/journals/american-political-science-review/article/activists-and-partisan-realignment-in-the-united-states/4B1BA26284250B56F13224AD11D95CDC">"Activists and Partisan Realignment in the United States"</a>. <i>American Political Science Review</i>. <b>97</b> (2): 245–60. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1017%2FS0003055403000650">10.1017/S0003055403000650</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Serial_Number" title="International Standard Serial Number">ISSN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/1537-5943">1537-5943</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 9,</span> 2018</span>. <q>By 2000, however, the New Deal party alignment no longer captured patterns of partisan voting. In the intervening 40 years, the Civil Rights and Voting Rights Acts had triggered an increasingly race-driven distinction between the parties. [...] Goldwater won the electoral votes of five states of the Deep South in 1964, four of them states that had voted Democratic for 84 years (Califano 1991, 55). He forged a new identification of the Republican party with racial conservatism, reversing a century-long association of the GOP with racial liberalism. This in turn opened the door for Nixon's "Southern strategy" and the Reagan victories of the eighties.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=American+Political+Science+Review&amp;rft.atitle=Activists+and+Partisan+Realignment+in+the+United+States&amp;rft.volume=97&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.pages=245-60&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1017%2FS0003055403000650&amp;rft.issn=1537-5943&amp;rft.aulast=Miller&amp;rft.aufirst=Gary&amp;rft.au=Schofield%2C+Norman&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cambridge.org%2Fcore%2Fjournals%2Famerican-political-science-review%2Farticle%2Factivists-and-partisan-realignment-in-the-united-states%2F4B1BA26284250B56F13224AD11D95CDC&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADemocratic+Party+%28United+States%29" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
